**[10:158]** ) He might, it is true, if he had willed, have led a trader's or a merchant's life, and would surely not have lacked.

**[10:159]** But he would not.

**[10:160]** What then, says one, is he here profited? Tell me, what is he profited? Does he wear silken robes? Does he proudly clear his way through the forum with a troop of followers? Is he borne along on horseback? Does he build houses, having where to dwell? If he act so, I too accuse him, and spare him not, but declare that he is unworthy of the priesthood.

**[10:161]** For how can he exhort others not to spend their time on these superfluities, who cannot advise himself? But if he has sufficient for support, is he therefore doing wrong? Would you have him lead a vagabond life, and beg? Wouldest not thou too, his disciple, be put to shame? But if thy father in the flesh did this, thou wouldest think shame of the thing.

**[10:162]** If thy spiritual father be compelled so to do, wilt thou not veil thy head, and even think thou art sinking into the earth? It is written, "A father's dishonor is a reproach to the children".

**[10:163]** (Ecclus.

**[10:164]** iii.

**[10:165]** ) But what? Should he perish with famine? This were not like a pious man; for God willeth it not.

**[10:166]** But what do they straightway philosophize? It is written, say they, "Get you no gold, nor silver, nor brass in your purses, neither two coats, nor yet staves" (Matt.

**[10:167]** x.

**[10:168]** ,.

**[10:169]** ), whilst these men have three or four garments, and beds well spread.

**[10:170]** I am forced now to heave a bitter sigh, and, but that it had been indecorous, I had wept too! How so? Because we are such curious searchers into the motes of others, while we feel not the beams in our own eyes.

**[10:171]** Tell me, why sayest thou not this to thyself? The answer is, Because the command is laid only on our Teachers.

**[10:172]** When then Paul says, "having food and covering we shall be therewith content" ( Tim.

**[10:173]** vi.

**[10:174]** ), says he this only to Teachers? By no means, but to all men; and this is clear, if we will begin farther back.

**[10:175]** For what does he say? "Godliness with contentment is great gain ( Tim.

**[10:176]** vi.

**[10:177]** ); for we brought nothing into this world, it is certain that neither can we carry anything out" ( Tim.

**[10:178]** vi.

**[10:179]** ); he then straightway adds, "And having food and covering, we shall be therewith content; but they that desire to be rich, fall into a temptation and a snare, and many foolish and hurtful lusts".

**[10:180]** ( Tim.

**[10:181]** vi.

**[10:182]** ,.

**[10:183]** ) You see that this is spoken to all; and how is it when he says again, "Make not provision for the flesh to fulfill the lusts thereof" (Rom.

**[10:184]** xiii.

**[10:185]** ), is not this said absolutely to all? and what when he says, "Meats for the belly, and the belly for meats, but God shall bring to nought both it and them" ( Cor.

**[10:186]** vi.

**[10:187]** ); or what when he says, "But she that giveth herself to pleasure, is dead while she liveth" ( Tim.

**[10:188]** v.

**[10:189]** ), speaking of a widow.

**[10:190]** Is then the widow a Teacher? Has not he said himself, "But I permit not a woman to teach, nor to have dominion over a man"? ( Tim.

**[10:191]** ii.

**[10:192]** ) But if a widow, in old age, (and age has need of great attendance,) and a woman's nature too, (for the woman's sex, being weak, has need of more refreshment,) if then, where there is both the age and the nature, he suffers her not to live in luxury, but even says that she is dead, (for he did not simply forbid a life of luxury, but said, "she who giveth herself to luxury is dead while she liveth,") and thus hath cut her off, (for she that is dead is cut off,) what indulgence then will any man have, who does those things, for which a woman and an aged one too is punished?  Yet no one gives a thought to these things, no one searches them out.

**[10:193]** And this I have been compelled to say, not from any wish to free the priests from these charges, but to spare you.

**[10:194]** They indeed suffer no harm at your hands, even if it is with cause and justice that they are thus charged of being greedy of gain; for, whether ye speak, or whether ye forbear, they must there give an account to the Judge, so that your words hurt them not at all; but if your words are false besides, they for their part gain by these false accusations, whilst ye hurt yourselves by these means.

**[10:195]** But it is not so with you; for be the things true, which ye bring against them, or be they false, ye speak ill of them to your hurt.

**[10:196]** And how so? If they be true, in that ye judge your Teachers, and subvert order, ye do it to your hurt.

**[10:197]** For if we must not judge a brother, much less a Teacher.

**[10:198]** But if they be false, the punishment and retribution is intolerable; for of "every idle word ye shall give account".

**[10:199]** (Matt.

**[10:200]** xii.

**[10:201]** ) For your sake then I thus act and labor.

**[10:202]** But as I said, no one searches out these things, no one busies himself about these things, no one communes with himself on any of these things.

**[10:203]** Would ye that I should add still more? "Whosoever forsaketh not all that he hath, saith the Christ, is not worthy of Me".

**[10:204]** (Luke xiv.

**[10:205]** ; Matt.

**[10:206]** x.

**[10:207]** ) What when he says, "It is hard for a rich man to enter into the kingdom of heaven"? (Matt.

**[10:208]** xix.

**[10:209]** ; Mark x.

**[10:210]** ) What when he says again, "Woe unto you that are rich, for ye have received your consolation"? (Luke vi.

**[10:211]** ) No one searches this out, no one bears it in mind, no one reasons with himself, but all sit as severe inquisitors on other men's cases.

**[10:212]** Yet this is to make themselves sharers in the charges.

**[10:213]** But listen, that for your own sake I may free the priests from the charges, which ye say lie against them, for the persuasion that they transgress the law of God, inclines you not a little towards evil.

**[10:214]** Come then, let us examine this matter.

**[10:215]** Christ said, "Provide neither gold nor silver, neither two coats, neither shoes, nor girdle, nor yet staves".

**[10:216]** (Matt.

**[10:217]** x.

**[10:218]** ,.

**[10:219]** ) What then? tell me, did Peter transgress this command? Surely he did so, in having a girdle and a garment, and shoes, for listen to the words of the Angel, "Gird thyself, and bind on thy sandals".

**[10:220]** (Acts xii.

**[10:221]** ) And yet he had no such great need of sandals, for at that season a man may go even unshod; their great use is in the winter, and yet he had them.

**[10:222]** What shall we say of Paul, when he writes thus to Timothy, "Do thy diligence to come before winter"? ( Tim.

**[10:223]** iv.

**[10:224]** ) He gives him orders too and says, "The cloak that I left at Troas with Carpus bring when thou comest, and the books, especially the parchments".

**[10:225]** ( Tim.

**[10:226]** iv.

**[10:227]** ) See he speaks of a cloak, and no one can say that he had not another which he wore; for if he did not wear one at all, it were superfluous to order this one to be brought, and if he could not be without one to wear, it is clear he had a second.

**[10:228]** What shall we say of his remaining "two whole years in his own hired dwelling"? (Acts xxviii.

**[10:229]** ) Did then this chosen vessel disobey Christ? this man who said, "Yet I live; and yet no longer I, but Christ liveth in me" (Gal.

**[10:230]** ii.

**[10:231]** ), concerning whom Christ testified, saying, "He is a chosen vessel unto Me"? (Acts ix.

**[10:232]** ) I ought to leave this difficulty with you, without supplying any solution to the question.

**[10:233]** I ought to exact of you this penalty for your negligence in the Scriptures, for this is the origin of all such difficulties.

**[10:234]** For we know not the Scriptures, we are not trained in the law of God, and so we become sharp inquirers into the faults of others, whilst we take no account of our own.

**[10:235]** I ought then to have exacted from you this penalty.

**[10:236]** But what shall I do? Fathers freely give to their sons many things beyond what is fitting: when their fatherly compassion is kindled, on seeing their child with downcast look, and wasted with grief, they themselves also feel sharper pangs than he, and rest not until they have removed the ground of his dejection.

**[10:237]** So be it at least here, be ye at least dejected at not receiving, that ye may receive well.

**[10:238]** What then is it? They opposed not, far be it; but diligently followed the commands of Christ, for those commands were but for a season, and not enduring; and this I say not from conjecture, but from the divine Scriptures.

**[10:239]** And how? Luke relates that Christ said to His disciples, "When I sent you forth without purse, and wallet, and girdle, and shoes, lacked ye anything? And they said nothing.

**[10:240]** (Luke xxii.

**[10:241]** ) But for the future provide them".

**[10:242]** But tell me, what could he do? could he have but one coat? How then? If need was that this be washed, should he, because without it, stay at home? should he without it go abroad in an unbecoming manner, when need called? Consider what a thing it would have been that Paul, who made the circuit of the world with such great success, should remain at home for want of raiment, and thus hinder his noble work.

**[10:243]** And what if violent cold had set in, or rain had drenched it, or perhaps frozen in, how could he dry his raiment? must he again remain without it? And what if cold had deprived his body of strength? must he waste away with disease, and be unable to speak? For hear what he says to Timothy, to prove that they were not furnished with adamantine bodies, "Use a little wine for thy stomach's sake, and thine often infirmities".

**[10:244]** ( Tim.

**[10:245]** v.

**[10:246]** ) And again, when he speaks of another, "I counted it necessary to send to you your messenger, and minister to my needs".

**[10:247]** (Philip.

**[10:248]** ii.

**[10:249]** ) "For indeed he was sick, nigh unto death; but God had mercy on him, and not on him only, but on me also".

**[10:250]** (Philip.

**[10:251]** ii.

**[10:252]** ) So that they were subject to every sort of sickness.

**[10:253]** What then? must they die? By no means.

**[10:254]** For what cause then did Christ at that time give them that command? To show His own power, and to prove that in after times He was able to do it, though He did it not.

**[10:255]** But wherefore did He not do it? They were much more admirable than the Israelites, whose shoes did not wax old, neither their garments, and that too whilst they were journeying through that desert where the glowing rays of the sun strike so hot, that they are capable of consuming even stones.

**[10:256]** (Ref.

**[10:257]** to Deut.

**[10:258]** xxix.

**[10:259]** ) Why then did he do this? For thy sake.

**[10:260]** For since thou wouldest not remain in health, but be full of wounds, He gave you that which might serve for medicine.

**[10:261]** And this is hence manifest; could He not Himself have fed them? He that gave to thee, who wast an enemy with Him, would He not much more have given to Paul? He who gave to the Israelites, those murmurers, those fornicators, those idolaters, would He not much more have given to Peter, who spent all for His sake? He who suffered wicked men to possess aught, would He not much more have freely given to John, who for Him forsook even his father? Yet he would not: through your hands he feeds them, that you may be sanctified.

**[10:262]** And see the excess of His lovingkindness.

**[10:263]** He chose that His disciples should be in want, that thou mightest be a little refreshed.

**[10:264]** For if He had freed them from all want, they would have been much more admirable, far more glorious.

**[10:265]** But then that which is to thee salvation would have been cut off.

**[10:266]** God willed not then that they should be admirable, that thou mightest be saved, but that they should rather be lowered.

**[10:267]** He hath suffered them to be less accounted of, that thou mightest be able to be saved.

**[10:268]** The Teacher who receiveth is not equally reverenced, but he who receives not is chiefly honored.

**[10:269]** But then in the latter case the disciple is not benefited, he is hindered of his fruit.

**[10:270]** Seest thou the wisdom of God who thus loveth man? For as He Himself sought not His own glory, nor had respect to Himself, but when He was in glory, chose to be dishonored for thy sake, thus too is it in the case of your Teachers.

**[10:271]** When they might have been highly reverenced, He preferred that they should be subject to contempt for thy sake, that thou mightest be able to profit, that thou mightest be able to be rich.

**[10:272]** For he is in want of the things of this life, that you may abound in things spiritual.

**[10:273]** If then He might have made them above all want, He showed that for thy sake He suffers them to be in want.

**[10:274]** Knowing then these things, let us turn ourselves to well doing, not to accusations.

**[10:275]** Let us not be overcurious about the failings of others, but take account of our own; let us reckon up the excellences of other men, while we bear in mind our faults; and thus shall we be well pleasing to God.

**[10:276]** For he who looks at the faults of others, and at his own excellences, is injured in two ways; by the latter he is carried up to arrogance, through the former he falls into listlessness.

**[10:277]** For when he perceives that such an one hath sinned, very easily will he sin himself; when he perceives that he hath in aught excelled, very easily becometh he arrogant.

**[10:278]** He who consigns to oblivion his own excellences, and looks at his failings only, whilst he is a curious enquirer of the excellences, not the sins, of others, is profited in many ways.

**[10:279]** And how? When he sees that such an one hath done excellently, he is raised to emulate the same; when he sees that he himself hath sinned, he is rendered humble and modest.

**[10:280]** If we act thus, if we thus regulate ourselves, we shall be able to obtain the good things which are promised, through the grace and lovingkindness of our Lord Jesus Christ, with whom, &c.

**[11:1]** Homily X.

**[11:2]** Philippians iii.

**[11:3]** –  "Finally, my brethren, rejoice in the Lord.

**[11:4]** To write the same things to you, to me indeed is not irksome, but for you it is safe.

**[11:5]** Beware of the dogs, beware of the evil workers, beware of the concision.

**[11:6]** For we are the circumcision, who worship God in the spirit, and glory in Christ Jesus, and have no confidence in the flesh".

**[11:7]** Dejection and care, whenever they strain the soul beyond due measure, bereave it of its native force.

**[11:8]** And therefore Paul relieves the Philippians, who were in great despondency, and they were in despondency because they did not know how matters were with Paul; they were in despondency because they thought that it was already over with him, because of the preaching, because of Epaphroditus.

**[11:9]** It is in giving them assurance on all these points that he introduces the words, "Finally, my brethren, rejoice".

**[11:10]** "You no longer have," he says, "cause for despondency.

**[11:11]** You have Epaphroditus, for whose sake you were grieved; you have Timothy; I am myself coming to you; the Gospel is gaining ground.

**[11:12]** What is henceforth wanting to you? Rejoice!"  Now he calls the Galatians indeed "children" (Gal.

**[11:13]** iv.

**[11:14]** ), but these "brethren".

**[11:15]** For when he aims either to correct anything or to show his fondness, he calls them "children"; but when he addresses them with greater honor, "brethren" is the title.

**[11:16]** "Finally, my brethren," he says, "rejoice in the Lord".

**[11:17]** He said rightly "in the Lord," not "after the world".

**[11:18]** for this is no rejoicing.

**[11:19]** These tribulations, he says, which are according to Christ bring joy.

**[11:20]** "To write the same things to you, to me indeed is not irksome, but for you it is safe.

**[11:21]** Beware of the dogs".

**[11:22]** Dost thou mark how he forbears to bring in the exhortation at the beginning? But after he had given them much commendation, after he had shown his admiration of them, then he does this, and again repeats his commendation.

**[11:23]** For this mode of speech seems to bear somewhat hard upon them.

**[11:24]** Wherefore he overshadows it on every side.

**[11:25]** But whom does he style "dogs"? There were at this place some of those, whom he hints at in all his Epistles, base and contemptible Jews, greedy of vile lucre and fond of power, who, desiring to draw aside many of the faithful, preached both Christianity and Judaism at the same time, corrupting the Gospel.

**[11:26]** As then they were not easily discernible, therefore he says, "beware of the dogs": the Jews are no longer children; once the Gentiles were called dogs, but now the Jews.

**[11:27]** Wherefore? because as the Gentiles were strangers both to God and to Christ, even so are these become this now.

**[11:28]** And he shows forth their shamelessness and violence, and their infinite distance from the relation of children, for that the Gentiles were once called "dogs," hear what the Canaanitish woman says, "Yea, Lord: for even the dogs eat of the crumbs which fall from their masters' table".

**[11:29]** (Matt.

**[11:30]** xv.

**[11:31]** ) But that they might not have this advantage, since even dogs are at the table, he adds that, whereby he makes them aliens also, saying, "Beware of the evil workers"; he admirably expressed himself, "beware of the evil workers"; they work, he means, but for a bad end, and a work that is much worse than idleness, plucking up what is laid in goodly order.

**[11:32]** "Beware," he says, "of the concision".

**[11:33]** The rite of circumcision was venerable in the Jews' account, forasmuch as the Law itself gave way thereto, and the Sabbath was less esteemed than circumcision.

**[11:34]** For that circumcision might be performed the Sabbath was broken; but that the Sabbath might be kept, circumcision was never broken; and mark, I pray, the dispensation of God.

**[11:35]** This is found to be even more solemn than the Sabbath, as not being omitted at certain times.

**[11:36]** When then it is done away, much more is the Sabbath.

**[11:37]** Wherefore Paul makes a concision of the name, and says, "Beware of the concision"; and he did not say "that circumcision is evil, that it is superfluous," lest he should strike the men with dismay, but he manages it more wisely, withdrawing them from the thing, but gratifying them with the word, nay, rather with the thing too, in a more serious way.

**[11:38]** But not so in the case of the Galatians, for since in that case the disease was great, he forthwith adopts the remedy of amputation with open front and with all boldness; but in this case, as they had done nothing of the sort, he vouchsafes them the gratification of the title, he casts out the others, and says, "Beware of the concision; for we are the circumcision"—how?—"who worship God in spirit, and have no confidence in the flesh".

**[11:39]** He said not that "we test the one circumcision and the other, which is the better of the two"; but he would not even allow it a share in the name; but what does he say? That that circumcision is "concision".

**[11:40]** Why? Because they do nothing but cut the flesh up.

**[11:41]** For when what is done is not of the law, it is nothing else than a concision and cutting up of the flesh; it was then either for this reason that he called it so, or because they were trying to cut the Church in twain; and we call the thing "cutting up" in those who do this at random, without aim and without skill.

**[11:42]** Now if you must seek circumcision, he says, you will find it among us, "who worship God in spirit," i-e: who worship spiritually.

**[11:43]** For answer me, which is superior, the soul or the body? Evidently the former.

**[11:44]** Therefore that circumcision is also superior, or rather, no longer superior, but this is the only circumcision; for while the type stood, He rightly brought it forward in conjunction, writing, "For ye shall circumcise the foreskins of your hearts".

**[11:45]** (Jerem.

**[11:46]** iv.

**[11:47]** ) In the same way in the Epistle to the Romans he does away with it, saying, "for he is not a Jew which is one outwardly, neither is that circumcision which is outward in the flesh; but he is a Jew which is one inwardly, and circumcision is that of the heart, in the spirit, not in the letter".

**[11:48]** (Rom.

**[11:49]** ii.

**[11:50]** ,.

**[11:51]** ) And lastly, he takes from it the very name, "neither is it circumcision," he maintains; for the type while the reality is yet to come, is called this, but when the reality has come, it no longer retains the title.

**[11:52]** As in delineation, a man has drawn a king in outline; so long as the colors are not put on we say, Lo, there is the king, but when they are added, the type is lost in the reality, and ceases to show.

**[11:53]** And he said not, "for the circumcision is in us," but "we are the circumcision," and justly; for this is the Man, the circumcision in virtue, this is really the Man.

**[11:54]** And he did not say, "For among them is the concision"; for they themselves are henceforth in a condition of ruin and of wickedness.

**[11:55]** But no longer, says he, is circumcision performed in the body, but in the heart.

**[11:56]** "And have no confidence," says he, "in the flesh; though I myself might have confidence even in the flesh".

**[11:57]** (v.

**[11:58]** ) What does he call "confidence" here, and "in the flesh"? Boasting, boldness, a high tone.

**[11:59]** And he did well to add this; for if he had been of the Gentiles, and had condemned circumcision, and not only circumcision, but all those that adopted it out of place, it would have seemed that he was running it down, because he lacked the high ancestry of Judaism, as being a stranger to its solemn rites, and having no part therein.

**[11:60]** But as it is, he, who, though a sharer, yet blames them, will not therefore blame them as having no share in them, but as disowning them; not from ignorance, but most especially from acquaintance with them.

**[11:61]** Accordingly observe what he says in his Epistle to the Galatians also; having been brought into a necessity of saying great things about himself, how even in these circumstances does he manifest nought but humility.

**[11:62]** "For ye have heard, of my manner of life in time past," he says, "in the Jews' religion" (Gal.

**[11:63]** i.

**[11:64]** ); and again here; "if any other man thinketh to have confidence in the flesh, I more".

**[11:65]** (v.

**[11:66]** ).

**[11:67]** And he immediately added, "a Hebrew of Hebrews".

**[11:68]** But "if any other man," says he, showing the necessity, showing that it was on their account that he spoke.

**[11:69]** "If ye have confidence," he says, I also say so, since I am silent.

**[11:70]** And observe the absence of all ungraciousness in the reproofs; by forbearing to do it by name, he gave even them the opportunity of retracing their steps.

**[11:71]** "If any one thinketh to have confidence"; and it was well to say "thinketh," either inasmuch as they really had no such confidence, or as that confidence was no real confidence, for all was by necessity, and not of choice.

**[11:72]** "Circumcised on the eighth day"; and he sets down the first that wherein they chiefly boasted, viz.

**[11:73]** the ordinance of circumcision.

**[11:74]** "Of the stock of Israel".

**[11:75]** He pointed out both these circumstances, that he was neither a proselyte, nor born of proselytes; for from his being circumcised on the eighth day, it follows that he was not a proselyte, and from his being of the stock of Israel, that he was not of proselyte parents.

**[11:76]** But that you may not imagine that he was of the stock of Israel as coming of the ten tribes, he says, "of the tribe of Benjamin".

**[11:77]** So that he was of the more approved portion, for the place of the priests was in the lot of this tribe.

**[11:78]** "An Hebrew of Hebrews".

**[11:79]** Because he was not a proselyte, but from of old, of distinguished Jews; for he might have been of Israel, and yet not "an Hebrew of Hebrews," for many were already corrupting the matter, and were strangers to the language, being encircled by other nations; it is either this then, or the great superiority of his birth, that he shows.

**[11:80]** "According to the law a Pharisee".

**[11:81]** He is coming now to the circumstances dependent on his own will; for all those things were apart from the will, for his being circumcised was not of himself, nor that he was of the stock of Israel, nor that he was of the tribe of Benjamin.

**[11:82]** So that, even among these he has a larger share, even though there were really many who partook with him.

**[11:83]** Where then are we to place the "rather"? Particularly herein that he was not a proselyte; for to be of the most distinguished tribe and sect, and this from his ancestors of old, was a thing which belonged not to many.

**[11:84]** But he comes to the things which are matters of choice, wherein we have the "rather".

**[11:85]** "As touching the law, a Pharisee; as touching zeal, persecuting the Church".

**[11:86]** But this is not sufficient; for it is possible to be a Pharisee even, and yet not very zealous.

**[11:87]** But this also he adds; behold the "rather".

**[11:88]** "According to righteousness".

**[11:89]** It is possible, however, to be adventurous, or to act thus from ambition, and not out of zeal for the law, as the chief priests did.

**[11:90]** Yet neither was this the case, but, "according to the righteousness which is in the law, found blameless".

**[11:91]** If then both for purity of descent, and earnestness, and habits, and mode of life, I surpassed all, why have I renounced all those dignities, he asks, but because I found that the things of Christ are better, and better far? Wherefore he added; "howbeit what things were gain to me, these have I counted loss for Christ".

**[11:92]** (v.

**[11:93]** )  Such a course of life, so strictly regulated, and entered upon from earliest childhood, such unblemished extraction, such dangers, plots, labors, forwardness, did Paul renounce, "counting them but loss," which before were "gain," that he might "win Christ".

**[11:94]** But we do not even contemn money, that we may "win Christ," but prefer to fail of the life to come rather than of the good things of the present life.

**[11:95]** And yet this is nothing else than loss; for tell me now, let us examine in detail the conditions of riches, and see whether it be not loss accompanied with trouble, and without any gain.

**[11:96]** For tell me, what is the advantage of those stores of costly garments, what good do we gain when we are arrayed in them? None, nay, we are only losers.

**[11:97]** How so? Because even the poor man, in his cheap and threadbare clothing, does not bear the scorching in time of heat any wise worse than yourself; nay, rather he bears it better, for clothes that are threadbare and worn single allow more ease to the body, but not so with those which are new made, though they be finer than the spider's web.

**[11:98]** Besides, you, from your excessive self-importance, wear even two and often three inner garments, and a cloak and girdle, and breeches too, but no one blames him if he wears but a single inner garment; so that he is the man that endures most easily.

**[11:99]** It is owing to this that we see rich men sweating, but the poor subject to nothing of the sort.

**[11:100]** Since then his cheap clothing, which is sold for a trifle, answers the same or even a better purpose to him, and those clothes, which oblige a man to pay down much gold, do only the same thing, is not this great superabundance so much loss? For it has added nothing in respect of its use and service, but your purse is emptied of so much the more gold, and the same use and service.

**[11:101]** You who have riches have purchased for a hundred pieces of gold, or even more, but the poor man for a trifling sum of silver.

**[11:102]** Do you perceive the loss? No, for your pride will not let you see it.

**[11:103]** Would you have us make out this account in the case of the gold ornaments too, which men put alike about their horses and their wives? For besides the other evils, the possession of money makes fools of men; they account their wives and horses to be worthy of the same honor, and the ornamentation of both is the same; and they would make themselves finer by the same means as the very beasts that carry them, or as the very skins of the awnings, wherein they are borne.

**[11:104]** What now is the use of decking out a mule or a horse with gold? or the lady, that has such a weight of gold and jewels about her person, what does she gain? "But the golden ornaments are never worn out," he answers.

**[11:105]** Assuredly this also is said that in the baths and many places both precious stones and gold ornaments lose much of their value.

**[11:106]** But be it so, and grant that they are not injured, tell me, what is the gain? And how is it when they drop out, and are lost? is there no loss sustained? And how when they draw down upon you envy and intrigues? is there no loss then? For when they do the wearer no good, but rather inflame the eyes of the envious, and act as an incitement to the robber, do they not become loss? And again, say, when a man may use them for a serviceable purpose, but is unable on account of the extravagance of his wife, and is obliged to starve and to stint himself, that he may see her arrayed in gold, is it not a matter of loss? For it was on this account that goods have their name from use, not that we should use them thus like goldsmiths' samples, but that we should do some good therewith; so then when love of gold does not allow this, is not the whole thing loss? for he that dares not use them forbears the use as if they were another's property, and there is no use of them in any way.

**[11:107]** Again, how is it when we erect splendid and spacious mansions, decorated with columns, marbles, porticos, arcades, and in every possible way, setting images and statues everywhere? Many indeed even call demons out of these, i-e: the images, but let us omit the examination of those points.

**[11:108]** What too is the meaning of the gilded ceiling? Does it not supply the same need as to him, whose house is on a moderate scale? "But there is great delight in it," he says.

**[11:109]** Yes, for the first or second day, and afterwards, none at all, but it stands merely for nothing.

**[11:110]** For if the sun does not strike us with wonder, from its being customary, much more do works of art fail, and we only look at them like things of clay.

**[11:111]** For tell me, what does a range of pillars contribute to make your dwelling superior to others, or the finest statues, or the gilding spread over the wall? Nothing; rather, these come of luxury and insolence, and overweening pride and folly; for everything there ought to be necessary and useful, not superfluous things.

**[11:112]** Do you see that the thing is loss? Do you see that it is superfluous and unprofitable? for if it supplies no further use or delight, (and it "does," in the course of time, bring satiety,) it is nothing else than loss, and vainglory is the hindrance, which will not let us see this.

**[11:113]** Did Paul then forsake those things which he "counted gain," and shall not we even quit our loss, for Christ's sake? How long shall we be riveted to the earth? How long till we shall look up to heaven? Do ye not mark the aged, what little perception they have of the past? Do ye not mark those that are finishing their course, both men in age, and men in youth? Do ye not see persons in the midst of life bereft of them? Why are we so wedded to unstable objects? Why are we linked to things that are shifting? How long before we lay hold of the things that last? What would not the old give, were it granted them to divest themselves of their old age? How irrational then to wish to return to our former youth, and gladly to give everything for the sake of this, that we might become younger, and yet when it is ours to receive a youth that knows no old age, a youth too, which, joined with great riches, hath far more of spirit, to be unwilling to give up a little trifle, but to hold fast things that contribute not a whit to the present life.

**[11:114]** They can never rescue you from death, they have no power to drive away disease, to stay old age, or any one of those events, which happen by necessity and according to the law of nature.

**[11:115]** And do you still hold to them? Tell me, what do you gain? Drunkenness, gluttony, pleasures contrary to nature and various in kind, which are far worse torturers than the hardest masters.

**[11:116]** These are the advantages which we gain from riches, nor is there one besides, since we are not so minded, for if we had had the mind, we might have won heaven itself for our inheritance by our riches.

**[11:117]** "So then riches are good," he says.

**[11:118]** It is not riches, but the will of the possessor that effects this, for because it is the will that does this, it is in the power even of a poor man to win heaven.

**[11:119]** For, as I have often said, God does not regard the amount of the gifts, but the will of the givers; it is possible even for one in poverty, who has given but little, to bear off all, for God requires a measure proportioned to our ability, neither will riches secure heaven to us, nor poverty, hell; but a good or a bad will, either one or the other.

**[11:120]** This then let us correct, this let us repossess, this let us regulate, and all will then be easy to us.

**[11:121]** For as the artificer works the wood the same, whether his axe be of iron or of gold, or rather he does it the better with an implement of iron, so here too, the straight path of virtue is more easily kept in a state of poverty.

**[11:122]** For touching riches we read, "It is easier for a camel to go through a needle's eye, than for a rich man to enter into the kingdom of Heaven".

**[11:123]** (Matt.

**[11:124]** xix.

**[11:125]** ) But He has made no such declarations about poverty; nay, the very reverse.

**[11:126]** "Sell thy goods, and give to the poor, and come follow Me" (v.

**[11:127]** ); as if the act of following were to spring from the selling.

**[11:128]** Never then let us flee from poverty as an evil, for it is the procurer of heaven.

**[11:129]** Again, let us never follow riches as a good; for they are the ruin of such as walk unwarily, but in everything directing our eyes to God, let us, as occasion requires, use those gifts which He has vouchsafed us, both strength of body, and abundance of money, and every other gift; for it is unnatural that we, who have our being for Him, should make these things serviceable to others, yet not to Him who has made us.

**[11:130]** He formed thine eye: make it serviceable to Him, not to the devil.

**[11:131]** But how serviceable to Him? By contemplating His creatures and praising and glorifying Him, and by withdrawing it from all gaze at women.

**[11:132]** Did He make thy hands? Preserve them for His use, not for the devil, not putting them out for robbery and rapine, but for His commandments and for good deeds, for earnest prayers, for holding out help to the fallen.

**[11:133]** Hath He made thine ears? Give these to Him, and not to effeminate strains nor to disgraceful tales; but "let all thy communication be in the law of the Most High".

**[11:134]** (Ecclus.

**[11:135]** ix.

**[11:136]** ) For "stand," he says, "in the multitude of the elders, and whoever is wise, cleave unto him".

**[11:137]** (Ecclus.

**[11:138]** vi.

**[11:139]** ) Did he make thy mouth? Let it do nought that is displeasing to Him, but sing psalms, hymns, and spiritual songs.

**[11:140]** "Let no corrupt speech proceed out of your mouth," says the Apostle, "but such as is good for edifying as the need may be, that it may give grace to them that hear" (Eph.

**[11:141]** iv.

**[11:142]** ); for edification and not for subversion, for fair words and not for evil speaking and plotting against other, but the very opposite.

**[11:143]** He hath made thy feet, not that thou shouldest run to do evil, but to do good.

**[11:144]** He made thy belly, not that thou shouldest cram it to bursting, but to practice lessons of wisdom.

**[11:145]** For the production of children, He implanted desire in thy mind, not for fornication, nor for adultery.

**[11:146]** He gave thee understanding, not to make of thee a blasphemer or a reviler, but that thou mightest be without falsehood.

**[11:147]** He gave us both money to be used on fitting occasion, and strength likewise to be used on fitting occasion.

**[11:148]** He instituted arts, that our present state of existence might be held together by them, not that we should separate ourselves from spiritual things, not that we should devote ourselves to the base arts but to the necessary ones, that we might minister to one another's good, and not that we should plot one against another.

**[11:149]** He gave us a roof, that it might afford shelter from the rain, and no more, not that it should be decked out with gold, while the poor man perishes with hunger.

**[11:150]** He gave clothing to cover us, not to make a display withal, not that things like these should have much gold lavished upon them, and that Christ should perish naked.

**[11:151]** He gave you a place of shelter, not that you should keep it to yourself, but to offer it to others also.

**[11:152]** He gave thee land, not that, cutting off the chief portion of it, you should spend the good gifts of God upon harlots, and dancers, and actors, and flute players, and harp players, but upon those that hunger and are in want.

**[11:153]** He gave you the sea to sail on, that you might not be wearied with journeying, not that you should pry into its depths, and bring up thence precious stones and all the other things of the same kind, nor that you should make this your business.

**[11:154]** "Why then are there precious stones?" he says.

**[11:155]** Nay, do you tell me why these stones are such, and why one class are regarded as of great value, while the others are more useful? For these may be conducive to building, but those to no purpose; and these are stronger than those.

**[11:156]** "But they," he says, "produce a beautiful effect".

**[11:157]** How so? it is a matter of fancy.

**[11:158]** Are they whiter? No, they are not whiter than pure white marble, nor nearly equal to it.

**[11:159]** But are they stronger? Not even this can be said for them.

**[11:160]** Well then, are they more useful? are they larger? Not even this.

**[11:161]** Whence then are they so admired, save from fancy? For if they are neither more beautiful, (for we shall find others more shining and more white,) nor useful, nor stronger, whence came they to be so admired? Was it not from mere fancy? Why then did God give them? They were not His gift, but it is your own imagination that they are anything great.

**[11:162]** "How is it, then," he answers, "that even the Scripture shows admiration of them?" So far it addresses itself to your fancy.

**[11:163]** As a master too in talking to a child often admires the same object as it does, when he desires to attract and engage it.

**[11:164]** Why do you aim at finery in your clothing? He clothed thee with a garment and with sandals.

**[11:165]** But where is there any reason for these things? "The judgments of God," he says, "are more to be desired than gold; yea than much fine gold".

**[11:166]** (Ps.

**[11:167]** xix.

**[11:168]** ) These, beloved, are of no use.

**[11:169]** Had they been of use, he would not have bidden us despise them.

**[11:170]** And for Holy Scripture, it speaks with reference to our notion, and this too is an instance of God's lovingkindness.

**[11:171]** "Why then," he asks, "did He give purple and the like?" These things are products of God's gift.

**[11:172]** For He has willed by other things also to show forth His own riches.

**[11:173]** And He gave you corn too by itself; but from this you make many things, cakes and sweetmeats, of every sort and variety, having much enjoyment.

**[11:174]** Pleasure and vainglory give rise to all these inventions.

**[11:175]** It pleased you to set them before everything.

**[11:176]** For if a foreigner or a rustic, who was ignorant of the land, should put the question, and, seeing your admiration, were to say, "Why do you admire these?" What have you to say? that they are fair to look at? But not so.

**[11:177]** Let us then give up such notions; let us lay hold of the things that are truly real.

**[11:178]** These are not, but simply pass away, only flowing past like a river.

**[11:179]** Wherefore I charge you, let us take our stand upon the rock, that we both escape being easily turned about, and that we may obtain the good things to come, by the grace and lovingkindness of our Lord Jesus Christ, with whom, &c.

**[12:1]** Homily XI.

**[12:2]** Philippians iii.

**[12:3]** –  "Howbeit what things were gain to me, these have I counted loss for Christ.

**[12:4]** Yea verily, and I counted all things to be loss for the excellency of the knowledge of Christ Jesus my Lord: for whom I suffered the loss of all things, and do count them but dung, that I may gain Christ and be found in Him, not having a righteousness of mine own, even that which is of the law, but that which is through faith in Christ, the righteousness which is of God".

**[12:5]** In our contests with heretics, we must make the attack with minds in vigor, that they may be able to give exact attention.

**[12:6]** I will therefore begin nay present discourse where the last ended.

**[12:7]** And what was that? Having enumerated every Jewish boast, both those from his birth, and those that were from choice, he added, "Howbeit, what things were gain to me, these have I counted to be loss for the excellency of the knowledge of Christ Jesus my Lord; for whom I suffered the loss of all things, and do count them but dung, that I may gain Christ".

**[12:8]** Here the heretics spring to their attack: for even this comes of the wisdom of the Spirit, to suggest to them hopes of victory, that they may undertake the fight.

**[12:9]** For if it had been spoken plainly, they would have acted here as they have done in other places, they would have blotted out the words, they would have denied the Scripture, when they were unable at all to look it in the face.

**[12:10]** But as in the case of fishes, that which can take them is concealed so that they may swim up, and does not lie open to view; this in truth hath come to pass here too.

**[12:11]** The Law, they say, is called "dung" by Paul, it is called "loss".

**[12:12]** He says, it was not possible to gain Christ except I "suffered" this "loss".

**[12:13]** All these things induced the heretics to accept this passage, thinking it to be favorable to them: but when they had taken it, then did he enclose them on all sides with his nets.

**[12:14]** For what do they themselves say? Lo! the Law is "loss," is "dung"; how then do ye say that it is of God?  But these very words are favorable to the Law, and how they are so, shall be hence manifest.

**[12:15]** Let us attend accurately to his very words.

**[12:16]** He said not, The Law is loss: but "I counted it loss".

**[12:17]** But when he spake of gain, he said not, I counted them, but "they were gain".

**[12:18]** But when he spake of loss he said, "I counted": and this rightly; for the former was naturally so, but the latter became so, from my opinion.

**[12:19]** "What then? Is it not so?" says he.

**[12:20]** It is loss for Christ.

**[12:21]** And how has the law become gain? And it was not counted gain, but was so.

**[12:22]** For consider how great a thing it was, to bring men, brutalized in their nature, to the shape of men.

**[12:23]** If the law had not been, grace would not have been given.

**[12:24]** Wherefore? Because it became a sort of bridge; for when it was impossible to mount on high from a state of great abasement, a ladder was formed.

**[12:25]** But he who has ascended has no longer need of the ladder; yet he does not despise it, but is even grateful to it.

**[12:26]** For it has placed him in such a position, as no longer to require it.

**[12:27]** And yet for this very reason, that he doth not require it, it is just that he should acknowledge his obligation, for he could not fly up.

**[12:28]** And thus is it with the Law, it hath led us up on high; wherefore it was gain, but for the future we esteem it loss.

**[12:29]** How? Not because it is loss, but because grace is far greater.

**[12:30]** For as a poor man, that was in hunger, as long as he has silver, escapes hunger, but when he finds gold, and it is not allowable to keep both, considers it loss to retain the former, and having thrown it away, takes the gold coin; so also here; not because the silver is loss, for it is not; but because it is impossible to take both at once, but it is necessary to leave one.

**[12:31]** Not the Law then is loss, but for a man to cleave to the Law, and desert Christ.

**[12:32]** Wherefore it is then loss when it leads us away from Christ.

**[12:33]** But if it sends us on to Him, it is no longer so.

**[12:34]** For this cause he saith "loss for Christ"; if for Christ, it is not so naturally.

**[12:35]** But why doth not the Law suffer us to come to Christ? For this very cause, he tells us, was it given.

**[12:36]** And Christ is the fulfilling of the Law, and Christ is the end of the Law.

**[12:37]** It doth suffer us if we will.

**[12:38]** "For Christ is the end of the Law".

**[12:39]** He who obeyeth the Law, leaves the Law itself.

**[12:40]** It suffers, if we take heed to it, but if we do not take heed, it suffers not.

**[12:41]** "Yea verily, and I have counted all things but loss".

**[12:42]** Why, he means, do I say this of the Law? Is not the world good? Is not the present life good? but if they draw me away from Christ, I count these things loss.

**[12:43]** Why? "for the excellency of the knowledge of Jesus Christ my Lord".

**[12:44]** For when the sun hath appeared, it is loss to sit by a candle: so that the loss comes by comparison, by the superiority of the other.

**[12:45]** You see that Paul makes a comparison from superiority, not from diversity of kind; for that which is superior, is superior to somewhat of like nature to itself.

**[12:46]** So that he shows the connection of that knowledge by the same means, by which he draws the superiority from the comparison.

**[12:47]** "For whom I suffered the loss of all things, and do count them dung, that I may gain Christ".

**[12:48]** It is not yet manifest, whether he speaks of the Law, for it is likely that he applies it to the things of this world.

**[12:49]** For when he says, "the things which were gain to me, those I have counted loss for Christ; yea verily," he adds, "I count all things loss".

**[12:50]** Although he said all things, yet it is things present; and if you wish it to be the Law too, not even so is it insulted.

**[12:51]** For dung comes from wheat, and the strength of the wheat is the dung, I mean, the chaff.

**[12:52]** But as the dung was useful in its former state, so that we gather it together with the wheat, and had there been no dung, there would have been no wheat, thus too is it with the Law.

**[12:53]** Seest thou, how everywhere he calls it "loss," not in itself, but for Christ.

**[12:54]** "Yea verily, and I count all things but loss".

**[12:55]** Wherefore again? "For the excellency of the knowledge (of Him), for whom I suffered the loss of all things".

**[12:56]** Again, "wherefore too I count all things to be loss, that I may gain Christ".

**[12:57]** See how, from every point, he lays hold of Christ as his foundation, and suffers not the Law to be anywhere exposed, or receive a blow, but guards it on every side.

**[12:58]** "And that I may be found in Him, not having a righteousness of mine own, even that which is of the Law".

**[12:59]** If he who had righteousness, ran to this other righteousness because his own was nothing, how much rather ought they, who have it not, to run to Him? And he well said, "a righteousness of mine own," not that which I gained by labor and toil, but that which I found from grace.

**[12:60]** If then he who was so excellent is saved by grace, much more are you.

**[12:61]** For since it was likely they would say that the righteousness which comes from toil is the greater, he shows that it is dung in comparison with the other.

**[12:62]** For otherwise I, who was so excellent in it, would not have cast it away, and run to the other.

**[12:63]** But what is that other? That which is from the faith of God, i-e: it too is given by God.

**[12:64]** This is the righteousness of God; this is altogether a gift.

**[12:65]** And the gifts of God far exceed those worthless good deeds, which are due to our own diligence.

**[12:66]** But what is "By faith that I may know Him"? So then knowledge is through faith, and without faith it is impossible to know Him.

**[12:67]** Why how? Through it we must "know the power of His resurrection".

**[12:68]** For what reason can demonstrate to us the Resurrection? None, but faith only.

**[12:69]** For if the resurrection of Christ, who was according to the flesh, is known by faith, how can the generation of the Word of God be comprehended by reasoning? For the resurrection is less than the generation.

**[12:70]** Why? Because of that there have been many examples, but of this none ever; for many dead arose before Christ, though after their resurrection they died, but no one was ever born of a virgin.

**[12:71]** If then we must comprehend by faith that which is inferior to the generation according to the flesh, how can that which is far greater, immeasurably and incomparably greater, be comprehended by reason? These things make the righteousness; this must we believe that He was able to do, but how He was able we cannot prove.

**[12:72]** For from faith is the fellowship of His sufferings.

**[12:73]** But how? Had we not believed, neither should we have suffered: had we not believed, that "if we endure with Him, we shall also reign with Him" ( Tim.

**[12:74]** ii.

**[12:75]** ), we should not have endured the sufferings.

**[12:76]** Both the generation and the resurrection is comprehended by faith.

**[12:77]** Seest thou, that faith must not be absolutely, but through good works; for he especially believes that Christ hath risen, who in like sort gives himself up to dangers, who hath fellowship with Him in His sufferings.

**[12:78]** For he hath fellowship with Him who rose again, with Him who liveth; wherefore he saith, "And may be found in Him, not having a righteousness of mine own, even that which is of the law, but that which is through faith in Christ, the righteousness which is of God by faith: that I may know Him, and the power of His resurrection, and the fellowship of His sufferings, becoming conformed unto His death; if by any means I may attain unto the resurrection from the dead".

**[12:79]** He saith, being made conformable unto His death, i-e: having fellowship; whereas He suffered from men, thus I too; wherefore he said, "becoming conformed" and again in another place, "and fill up on my part that which is lacking of the afflictions of Christ in my flesh".

**[12:80]** (Col.

**[12:81]** i.

**[12:82]** ) That is, these persecutions and sufferings work the image of His death, for He sought not His own, but the good of many.

**[12:83]** Therefore persecutions, and afflictions, and straits, ought not to disturb you, but ought even to make you glad, because through them we are "conformed to His death".

**[12:84]** As if he had said, We are molded to His likeness; as he says in another place, where he writeth, "bearing about in the body the dying of the Lord Jesus".

**[12:85]** ( Cor.

**[12:86]** iv.

**[12:87]** ) And this too comes from great faith.

**[12:88]** For we not only believe that He arose, but that after His resurrection also He hath great power: wherefore we travel the same road which He travelled, i-e: we become brethren to Him in this respect also.

**[12:89]** As if he had said, We become Christ's in this respect.

**[12:90]** O how great is the dignity of sufferings! We believe that we become "conformed to His death" through sufferings! For as in baptism, we were "buried with the likeness of His death," so here, with His death.

**[12:91]** There did he rightly say, "The likeness of His death" (Rom.

**[12:92]** vi.

**[12:93]** ,.

**[12:94]** ), for there we died not entirely, we died not in the flesh, to the body, but to sin.

**[12:95]** Since then a death is spoken of, and a death; but He indeed died in the body, whilst we died to sin, and there the Man died which He assumed, who was in our flesh, but here the man of sin; for this cause he saith, "the likeness of His death," but here, no longer the likeness of His death, but His death itself.

**[12:96]** For Paul, in his persecutions, no longer died to sin, but in his very body.

**[12:97]** Wherefore, he endured the same death.

**[12:98]** "If by any means," saith he, "I may attain unto the resurrection from the dead".

**[12:99]** What sayest thou? All men will have a share in that.

**[12:100]** "For we shall not all sleep, but we shall all be changed" ( Cor.

**[12:101]** xv.

**[12:102]** ), and shall all share not only in the Resurrection, but in incorruption.

**[12:103]** Some indeed to honor, but others as a means of punishment.

**[12:104]** If therefore all have a share in the Resurrection, and not in the Resurrection only, but also in incorruption, how said he, "If by any means I may attain," as if about to share in some especial thing? "For this cause," saith he, "I endure these things, if by any means I may attain unto the resurrection from the dead".

**[12:105]** For if thou hadst not died, thou wouldest not arise.

**[12:106]** What is it then? Some great thing seems here to be hinted at.

**[12:107]** So great was it, that he dared not openly assert it, but saith, "If by any means".

**[12:108]** I have believed in Him and His resurrection, nay, moreover, I suffer for Him, yet I am unable to be confident concerning the Resurrection.

**[12:109]** What resurrection doth he here mention? That which leads to Christ Himself.

**[12:110]** I said, that I believed in "Him, and in the power of His resurrection," and that I "have fellowship with His sufferings," and that I "become conformed to His death".

**[12:111]** Yet after all these things I am by no means confident; as he said elsewhere, "Let him that thinketh he standeth, take heed lest he fall".

**[12:112]** ( Cor.

**[12:113]** x.

**[12:114]** ) And again, "I fear lest by any means, after that I have preached to others, I myself should be rejected".

**[12:115]** ( Cor.

**[12:116]** ix.

**[12:117]** )  Ver.

**[12:118]** "Not that I have already obtained, or am already made perfect: but I press on, if so be that I may apprehend that for which also I was apprehended by Christ Jesus".

**[12:119]** "Not that I have already obtained".

**[12:120]** What means "already obtained"? He speaks of the prize, but if he who had endured such sufferings, he who was persecuted, he "who had in him the dying of the Lord Jesus," was not yet confident about that resurrection, what can we say? What meaneth, "if I may apprehend"? What he before said, "If I may attain to the resurrection of the dead".

**[12:121]** ( Cor.

**[12:122]** iv.

**[12:123]** ) If I may apprehend, he saith, His resurrection; i-e: if I may be able to endure so great things, if I may be able to imitate Him, if I may be able to become conformed to Him.

**[12:124]** For example, Christ suffered many things, He was spit upon, He was stricken, was scourged, at last He suffered what things he suffered.

**[12:125]** This is the entire course.

**[12:126]** Through all these things it is needful that men should endure the whole contest, and so come to His resurrection.

**[12:127]** Or he means this, if I am thought worthy to attain the glorious resurrection, which is a matter of confidence, in order to His resurrection.

**[12:128]** For if I am able to endure all the contests, I shall be able also to have His resurrection, and to rise with glory.

**[12:129]** For not as yet, saith he, am I worthy, but "I press on, if so be that I may apprehend".

**[12:130]** My life is still one of contest, I am still far from the end, I am still distant from the prize, still I run, still I pursue.

**[12:131]** And He said not, I run, but "I pursue".

**[12:132]** For you know with what eagerness a man pursues.

**[12:133]** He sees no one, he thrusts aside with great violence all who would interrupt his pursuit.

**[12:134]** He collects together his mind, and sight, and strength, and soul, and body, looking to nothing else than the prize.

**[12:135]** But if Paul, who so pursued, who had suffered so many things, yet saith, "if I may attain," what should we say, who have relaxed our efforts? Then to show that the thing is of debt, he saith, "For which also I was apprehended by Christ Jesus".

**[12:136]** I was, he saith, of the number of the lost, I gasped for breath, I was nigh dead, God apprehended me.

**[12:137]** For He pursued us, when we fled from Him, with all speed.

**[12:138]** So that he points out all those things; for the words, "I was apprehended," show the earnestness of Him who wishes to apprehend us, and our great aversion to Him, our wandering, our flight from Him.

**[12:139]** So that we are liable for a vast debt, and no one grieves, no one weeps, no one groans, all having returned to their former state.

**[12:140]** For as before the appearance of Christ we fled from God, so now also.

**[12:141]** For we can flee from God, not in place, for He is everywhere; and hear the Prophet, when he says, "Whither shall I go from Thy Spirit, or whither shall I flee from Thy presence"? (Ps.

**[12:142]** cxxxix.

**[12:143]** ) How then can we flee from God? Even as we can become distant from God, even as we can be removed afar off.

**[12:144]** "They that are far from Thee," it says, "shall perish".

**[12:145]** (Ps.

**[12:146]** lxxiii.

**[12:147]** ) And again, "Have not your iniquities separated between Me and you?" (Isa.

**[12:148]** lix.

**[12:149]** ) How then comes this removal, how comes this separation? In purpose and soul: for it cannot be in place.

**[12:150]** For how could one fly from Him who is everywhere present? The sinner then flies.

**[12:151]** This is what the Scripture saith, "The wicked fleeth when no man pursueth him".

**[12:152]** (Prov.

**[12:153]** xxviii.

**[12:154]** ) We eagerly fly from God, although He always pursueth us.

**[12:155]** The Apostle hasted, that he might be near Him.

**[12:156]** We haste, that we may be far off.

**[12:157]** Are not these things then worthy of lamentation? Are they not worthy of tears? Whither fliest thou, wretched and miserable man? Whither fliest thou from thy Life and thy Salvation? If thou fly from God, with whom wilt thou take refuge? If thou fly from the Light, whither wilt thou cast thine eyes? If thou fly from thy Life, whence wilt thou henceforth live? Let us fly from the enemy of our Salvation! Whensoever we sin we fly from God, we are as runaways, we depart to a foreign land, as he who consumed his paternal goods and departed into a foreign land, who wasted all his father's substance, and lived in want.

**[12:158]** We too have substance from our Father; and what is this? He hath freed us from our sins; He hath freely given to us power, strength for works of virtue; He hath freely given to us readiness, patience; He hath freely given to us the Holy Ghost in our baptism; if we waste these things we shall henceforth be in want.

**[12:159]** For as the sick, as long as they are troubled with fevers, and badness of their juices, are unable to arise or work, or do anything, but if any one sets them free, and brings them to health, if they then work not, this comes from their own sloth; thus too is it with us.

**[12:160]** For the disease was heavy and the fever excessive.

**[12:161]** And we lay not upon a bed, but upon wickedness itself, cast away in crime, as on a dunghill, full of sores, and evil odors, squalid, wasting away, more like ghosts than men.

**[12:162]** Evil spirits encompassed us about, the Prince of this world deriding and assaulting us; the Only-Begotten Son of God came, sent forth the rays of His Presence, and straightway dispelled the darkness.

**[12:163]** The King, who is on His Father's throne, came to us, having left His Father's throne.

**[12:164]** And when I say having left, think not of any removal, for He filleth the heavens and the earth, but I speak of the economy; He came to an enemy, who hated Him, who turned himself away, who could not endure to behold Him, who blasphemed Him every day.

**[12:165]** He saw him lying on a dunghill, eaten with worms, afflicted with fever and hunger, having every sort of disease; for both fever vexed him, which is evil desire; and inflammation lay heavy on him, this is pride; and gnawing hunger had hold of him, which is covetousness; and putrefying sores on every side, for this is fornication; and blindness of eyes, which is idolatry; and dumbness, and madness, which is to worship stocks and stones, and address them; and great deformity, for wickedness is this, foul to behold, and a most heavy disease.

**[12:166]** And he saw us speaking more foolishly than the mad, and calling stocks our God, and stones likewise; He saw us in such great guilt, he did not reject us; was not wroth, turned not away, hated us not, for He was a Master, and could not hate His own creation.

**[12:167]** But what does he do? As a most excellent physician, He prepareth medicines of great price, and Himself tastes them first.

**[12:168]** For He Himself first followed after virtue, and thus gave it to us.

**[12:169]** And He first gave us the washing, like some antidote, and thus we vomited up all our guilt, and all things took their flight at once, and our inflammation ceased, and our fever was quenched, and our sores were dried up.

**[12:170]** For all the evils which are from covetousness, and anger, and all the rest, were dissipated by the Spirit.

**[12:171]** Our eyes were opened, our ears were opened, our tongue spake holy words: our soul received strength, our body received such beauty and bloom, as it is like that he who is born a son of God should have from the grace of the Spirit; such glory as it is like that the new-born son of a king should have, nurtured in purple.

**[12:172]** Alas! How great nobility did He confer on us!  We were born, we were nurtured, why do we again fly from our Benefactor? He then, who hath done all these things, giveth us strength too, for it was not possible, for a soul bowed down by the disease to endure it, did not He Himself give us the strength.

**[12:173]** He gave us remission of our sins.

**[12:174]** We devoured all things.

**[12:175]** He gave us strength, we wasted it.

**[12:176]** He gave us grace, we quenched it; and how? we consumed it upon nought that was fitting, we used it for no useful end.

**[12:177]** These things have destroyed us, and what is more dreadful than all, when we are in a foreign country, and feeding on husks, we say not, Let us return to our Father, and say, "We have sinned against Heaven, and against Thee".

**[12:178]** (Luke xv.

**[12:179]** ) And that too, when we have so loving a Father, who eagerly desires our return.

**[12:180]** If we will only return to Him, He does not even bear to call in question our former deeds, only let us quit them.

**[12:181]** It is sufficient apology with Him, that we have returned.

**[12:182]** Not only He Himself calls not in question, but if another does so, He stops his mouth, though the accuser be one of good repute.

**[12:183]** Let us return! How long do we stand afar off? Let us perceive our dishonor, let us be sensible of our vileness.

**[12:184]** Sin makes us swine, sin brings famine to the soul; let us regain ourselves, and be sober again, and return to our former high birth, that we may obtain the good things which are to come, in Christ Jesus our Lord, with whom to the Father together with the Holy Spirit be glory, might, honor, now and ever and world without end.

**[13:1]** Homily XII.

**[13:2]** Philippians iii.

**[13:3]** ,  "Brethren, I count not myself yet to have apprehended: but one thing I do, forgetting the things which are behind, and stretching forward to the things which are before, I press on toward the goal unto the prize of the high calling of God in Christ Jesus".

**[13:4]** Nothing so renders our real excellences vain and puffs them away, as to be remembering the good deeds we have done; for this produces two evils, it both renders us remiss, and raises us to haughtiness.

**[13:5]** Wherefore see how Paul, since he knew our nature to be easily inclined to remissness, though he had given great praise to the Philippians, now subdues their mind by many other things above, but chiefly by his present words.

**[13:6]** And what are they? "Brethren, I count not myself to have apprehended".

**[13:7]** But if Paul had not as yet apprehended, and is not confident about the Resurrection and things to come, hardly should they be so, who have not attained the smallest proportion of his excellence.

**[13:8]** That is, I consider that I have not as yet apprehended all virtue, as if one were speaking of a runner.

**[13:9]** Not as yet, saith he, have I completed all.

**[13:10]** And if in another place he saith, "I have fought the good fight" ( Tim.

**[13:11]** iv.

**[13:12]** ), but here, "I count myself not as yet to have apprehended"; any one who reads carefully will well know the reason both of those, and of the present words; (for it is not necessary to dwell continually on the same point;) and that he spoke these words at a much earlier date, but the others near his death.

**[13:13]** But I am solely engaged on "one thing," says he, "in stretching forward to the things which are before".

**[13:14]** But "one thing," says he, "forgetting the things which are behind, and stretching forward to the things which are before, I press on toward the goal unto the prize of the high calling of God in Christ Jesus".

**[13:15]** For what made him reach forward unto the things which are before, was his forgetting the things that are behind.

**[13:16]** He then, who thinks that all is accomplished, and that nothing is wanting to him for the perfecting of virtue, may cease running, as having apprehended all.

**[13:17]** But he who thinks that he is still distant from the goal, will never cease running.

**[13:18]** This then we should always consider, even though we have wrought ten thousand good deeds; for if Paul, after ten thousand deaths, after so many dangers, considered this, how much more should we? For I fainted not, saith he, although I availed not, after running so much; nor did I despair, but I still run, I still strive.

**[13:19]** This thing only I consider, that I may in truth advance.

**[13:20]** Thus too we should act, we should forget our successes, and throw them behind us.

**[13:21]** For the runner reckons not up how many circuits he hath finished, but how many are left.

**[13:22]** We too should reckon up, not how far we are advanced in virtue, but how much remains for us.

**[13:23]** For what doth that which is finished profit us, when that which is deficient is not added? Moreover he did not say, I do not reckon up, but I do not even remember.

**[13:24]** For we thus become eager, when we apply all diligence to what is left, when we give to oblivion everything else.

**[13:25]** "Stretching forward," saith he; before we arrive, we strive to obtain.

**[13:26]** For he that stretches forward is one who, though his feet are running, endeavors to outstrip them with the rest of his body, stretching himself towards the front, and reaching out his hands, that he may accomplish somewhat more of the course.

**[13:27]** And this comes from great eagerness, from much warmth; thus the runner should run with great earnestness, with so great eagerness, without relaxation.

**[13:28]** As far as one who so runs differs from him who lies supine, so far doth Paul differ from us.

**[13:29]** He died daily, he was approved daily, there was no season, there was no time in which his course advanced not.

**[13:30]** He wished not to take, but to snatch the prize; for in this way we may take it.

**[13:31]** He who giveth the prize standeth on high, the prize is laid up on high.

**[13:32]** See how great a distance this is that must be run over! See how great an ascent! Thither we must fly up with the wings of the Spirit, otherwise it is impossible to surmount this height.

**[13:33]** Thither must we go with the body, for it is allowed.

**[13:34]** "For our citizenship is in heaven" (Philip.

**[13:35]** iii.

**[13:36]** ), there is the prize; seest thou the runners, how they live by rule, how they touch nothing that relaxes their strength, how they exercise themselves every day in the palaestra, under a master, and by rule? Imitate them, or rather exhibit even greater eagerness, for the prizes are not equal: many are those who would hinder you; live by rule: many are the things which relax your strength; make its feet agile: for it is possible so to do, it comes not naturally, but by our will.

**[13:37]** Let us bring it to lightness, lest our swiftness of foot be hindered by the weight of other things.

**[13:38]** Teach thy feet to be sure, for there are many slippery places, and if thou fallest, straightway thou losest much.

**[13:39]** But yet if thou fall, rise up again.

**[13:40]** Even thus mayst thou obtain the victory.

**[13:41]** Never attempt slippery things, and thou wilt not fall; walk upon firm ground, up with thy head, up with thine eyes; these commands the trainers give to those who run.

**[13:42]** Thus thy strength is supported; but if thou stoopest downward, thou fallest, thou art relaxed.

**[13:43]** Look upward, where the prize is; the sight of the prize increaseth the determination of our will.

**[13:44]** The hope of taking it suffereth not to perceive the toils, it maketh the distance appear short.

**[13:45]** And what is this prize? No palm branch; but what? The kingdom of heaven, everlasting rest, glory together with Christ, the inheritance, brotherhood, ten thousand good things, which it is impossible to name.

**[13:46]** It is impossible to describe the beauty of that prize; he who hath it alone knoweth it, and he who is about to receive it.

**[13:47]** It is not of gold, it is not set with jewels, it is far more precious.

**[13:48]** Gold is mire, in comparison with that prize, precious stones are mere bricks in comparison with its beauty.

**[13:49]** If thou hast this, and takest thy departure to heaven, thou wilt be able to walk there with great honor; the angels will reverence thee, when thou bearest this prize, with much confidence wilt thou approach them all.

**[13:50]** "In Christ Jesus".

**[13:51]** See the humility of his mind; this I do, saith he, "in Christ Jesus," for it is impossible without an impulse from Him to pass over so vast an interval: we have need of much aid, of a mighty alliance; He hath willed that thou shouldest struggle below, on high He crowns thee.

**[13:52]** Not as in this world; the crown is not here, where the contest is; but the crown is in that bright place.

**[13:53]** See ye not, even here, that the most honored of the wrestlers and charioteers are not crowned in the course below, but the king calls them up, and crowns them there? Thus too is it here, in heaven thou receivest the prize.

**[13:54]** Ver.

**[13:55]** "Let us, therefore, as many as be perfect, be thus minded," saith he.

**[13:56]** "And if in anything ye are otherwise minded, even this shall God reveal unto you".

**[13:57]** What sort of thing? That we should "forget the things which are behind".

**[13:58]** Wherefore it belongs to him who is perfect not to consider himself perfect.

**[13:59]** How therefore sayest thou, "as many as are perfect"? For tell me, are we minded as thou art? For if thou hast not attained nor art perfected, how dost thou command those that are perfect to be so minded as thou art, who art not yet perfect? Yea, for this, saith he, is perfection.

**[13:60]** And "if ye are in anything otherwise minded, even this shall God reveal unto you".

**[13:61]** That is, if any one considers that he has attained all excellence.

**[13:62]** He puts them on their guard, not by speaking directly, but what saith he? "If in anything ye are otherwise minded, even this shall God reveal unto you".

**[13:63]** See how humbly he saith this! God shall teach you, i-e: God shall persuade you, not teach you; for Paul was teaching, but God shall lead them on.

**[13:64]** And he said not, shall lead you on, but "shall reveal," that this may rather seem to spring from ignorance.

**[13:65]** These words were spoken not concerning doctrines, but concerning perfection of life, and our not considering ourselves to be perfect, for he who considers that he hath apprehended all, hath nothing.

**[13:66]** Ver.

**[13:67]** "Only, whereunto we have already attained, by that same rule let us walk, let us mind the same thing".

**[13:68]** "Only, whereunto we have attained".

**[13:69]** What means this? Let us hold fast, he saith, that in which we have succeeded; love, concord, and peace: for in this we have succeeded.

**[13:70]** "Whereto we have attained: to walk by the same rule, to mind the same thing".

**[13:71]** "Whereunto we have attained," i-e: in this we have already succeeded.

**[13:72]** Seest thou, that he wills that his precepts should be a rule to us? And a rule admits neither addition, nor subtraction, since that destroys its being a rule.

**[13:73]** "By the same rule," i-e: by the same faith, within the same limits.

**[13:74]** Ver.

**[13:75]** "Brethren, be ye imitators of me, and mark them which so walk even as ye have us for an ensample".

**[13:76]** He had said above, "beware of dogs," from such he had led them away; he brings them near to these whom they ought to imitate.

**[13:77]** If any one, saith he, wishes to imitate me, if any one wishes to walk the same road, let him take heed to them; though I am not present, ye know the manner of my walk, that is, my conduct in life.

**[13:78]** For not by words only did he teach, but by deeds too; as in the chorus, and the army, the rest must imitate the leader of the chorus or the army, and thus advance in good order.

**[13:79]** For it is possible that the order may be dissolved by sedition.

**[13:80]** The Apostles therefore were a type, and kept throughout a certain archetypal model.

**[13:81]** Consider how entirely accurate their life was, so that they are proposed as an archetype and example, and as living laws.

**[13:82]** For what was said in their writings, they manifested to all in their actions.

**[13:83]** This is the best teaching; thus he will be able to carry on his disciple.

**[13:84]** But if he indeed speaks as a philosopher, yet in his actions doth the contrary, he is no longer a teacher.

**[13:85]** For mere verbal philosophy is easy even for the disciple: but there is need of that teaching and leading which comes of deeds.

**[13:86]** For this both makes the teacher to be reverenced, and prepares the disciple to yield obedience.

**[13:87]** How so? When one sees him delivering philosophy in words, he will say he commands impossibilities; that they are impossibilities, he himself is the first to show, who does not practice them.

**[13:88]** But if he sees his virtue fully carried out in action, he will no longer be able to speak thus.

**[13:89]** Yet although the life of our teacher be careless, let us take heed to ourselves, and let us listen to the words of the prophet; "They shall be all taught of God".

**[13:90]** (Isa.

**[13:91]** liv.

**[13:92]** ) "And they shall teach no more every man his brother, saying, Know the Lord, for they shall all know me from the least of them to the greatest of them".

**[13:93]** (Jer.

**[13:94]** xxxi.

**[13:95]** ) Hast thou a teacher who is not virtuous? Still thou hast Him who is truly a Teacher, whom alone thou shouldest call a Teacher.

**[13:96]** Learn from Him: He hath said, "Learn of Me, for I am meek".

**[13:97]** (Matt.

**[13:98]** xi.

**[13:99]** ) Take not heed, then, to thy teacher, but to Him and to His lessons.

**[13:100]** Take thence thy examples, thou hast a most excellent model, to it conform thyself.

**[13:101]** There are innumerable models laid before thee in the Scriptures of virtuous lives; whichsoever thou wilt, come, and after the Master find it in the disciples.

**[13:102]** One hath shown forth through poverty, another through riches; for example, Elijah through poverty, Abraham through riches.

**[13:103]** Go to that example, which thou esteemest most easy, most befitting thyself to practice.

**[13:104]** Again, one by marriage, the other by virginity; Abraham by marriage, the other by virginity.

**[13:105]** Follow whichever thou wilt: for both lead to heaven.

**[13:106]** One shone forth by fasting, as John, another without fasting, as Job.

**[13:107]** Again, this latter had a care for his wife, his children, his daughters, his family, and possessed great wealth; the other possessed nothing but the garment of hair.

**[13:108]** And why do I make mention of family, or wealth, or money, when it is possible that even one in a kingdom should lay hold on virtue, for the house of a king would be found more full of trouble than any private family.

**[13:109]** David then shone forth in his kingdom; the purple and the diadem rendered him not at all remiss.

**[13:110]** To another it was entrusted to preside over a whole people, I mean Moses, which was a more difficult task, for there the power was greater, whence the difficulty too became greater.

**[13:111]** Thou hast seen men approved in wealth, thou hast seen them in poverty also, thou hast seen them in marriage, thou hast seen them in virginity too; on the contrary, behold some lost in marriage and in virginity, in wealth and in poverty.

**[13:112]** For example, many men have perished in marriage, as Samson, yet not from marriage, but from their own deliberate choice.

**[13:113]** Likewise in virginity, as the five virgins.
